<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Post-classical history</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Period between ancient history and modern history</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Post-classical" redirects here. For the film editing style commonly used in American films since the 1960s, see <a href="/wiki/Post-classical_editing" title="Post-classical editing">Post-classical editing</a>. For the period of Maya history, see <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization#Postclassic_period_(c._950–1539_AD)" title="Maya civilization">Maya civilization § Postclassic period (c. 950–1539 AD)</a>.</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="36" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e0/Merge-split-transwiki_default.svg/36px-Merge-split-transwiki_default.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e0/Merge-split-transwiki_default.svg/54px-Merge-split-transwiki_default.svg.png 1.5x, class="mbox-text-span">A request that this article title be changed is <a href="/wiki/Talk:Post-classical_history#Requested_move_6_October_2022" title="Talk:Post-classical history">under Please <b>do not move</b> this article until the discussion is closed.</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jingangjing.jpg"><img alt="Diamond Sutra, World's first printed book" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3438" data-file-width="4917" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Jingangjing.jpg/300px-Jingangjing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Jingangjing.jpg/450px-Jingangjing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Jingangjing.jpg/600px-Jingangjing.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jingangjing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Sutra" title="Diamond Sutra">Diamond Sutra</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang" title="Dunhuang">Dunhuang</a>, China was published in 868 AD as the first printed book using <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">woodblock printing</a> techniques. This image is a front piece for the book which was instrumental in spreading <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_Buddhism" title="East Asian Buddhism">East-Asian Buddhism</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>). Post-classical times were an era of religion. Matters of faith took part in the development of political power and in the personal lives of most ordinary people in both the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>. Geographic regions were often divided based on a location's religious affiliation.</div></div></div>

</div></td>
</tr></tbody></table></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below" style="border-top:1px solid #aaa;background:#f8eaba;text-align:left;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;">
<span style="font-size: 120%;">↓</span> <a href="/wiki/Future" title="Future">Future</a> <span class="nowrap">  </span></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="background:#f8eaba;padding:0.4em;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_history" title="Template:Human history"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Human_history" title="Template talk:Human history"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Human_history&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Post-classical history</b>, as used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_history" title="Global history">global history</a>, generally runs from about 500 AD to 1500 AD (roughly corresponding to the European <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>). The period is characterized by the expansion of civilizations geographically and development of trade networks between civilizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weller-stearns_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-weller-stearns-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[A]</a></sup> This period is also called the <b>medieval era</b>, <b>post-antiquity era</b>, <b>post-ancient era</b>, or <b>pre-modern era</b>.
</p><p>In Asia, the <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Islam" title="Spread of Islam">spread of Islam</a> created a <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">new empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a> with trade among the <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> continents, and advances in <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Science in the medieval Islamic world">science in the medieval Islamic world</a>. <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> experienced the full establishment of power of <a href="/wiki/History_of_China#Imperial_China" title="History of China">Imperial China</a>, which established several prosperous dynasties influencing Korea, Vietnam, and Japan. Religions such as <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a> spread in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">Gunpowder</a> was developed in China during the post-classical era. The <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> connected Europe and Asia, creating safe trade and stability between the two regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> In total the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_population_estimates" title="World population estimates">population of the world</a> doubled in the time period from approximately 210 million in 500 AD to 461 million in 1500 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup> Population generally grew steadily throughout the period but endured some incidental declines in events including the <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">Plague of Justinian</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_and_conquests" title="Mongol invasions and conquests">Mongol invasions</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haub1650_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haub1650-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology_and_periodization">Terminology and periodization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Terminology and periodization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leonardo_Bruni_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="408" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Leonardo_Bruni_2.jpg/170px-Leonardo_Bruni_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Leonardo_Bruni_2.jpg/255px-Leonardo_Bruni_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/12/Leonardo_Bruni_2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leonardo_Bruni_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Bruni" title="Leonardo Bruni">Leonardo Bruni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> historian who helped develop the concept of "<a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>"</div></div></div>
<p>Post-classical history is a <a href="/wiki/Periodization" title="Periodization">periodization</a> used by historians employing a <a href="/wiki/World_history" title="World history">world history</a> approach to history, specifically the school developed during the late 20th and early 21st centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weller-stearns_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-weller-stearns-3">[3]</a></sup> Outside of world history, the term is also sometimes used to avoid erroneous pre-conceptions around the terms <i>Middle Ages</i>, <i>Medieval</i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">Dark Ages</a></i> (see <a href="/wiki/Medievalism" title="Medievalism">Medievalism</a>), though the application of the term <i>post-classical</i> on a global scale is also problematic, and may likewise be <a href="/wiki/Eurocentrism" title="Eurocentrism">Eurocentric</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> Academic publications sometimes use the terms post-classical and late antiquity synomously to describe the history of Western Eurasia between 250 and 800<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The post-classical period corresponds roughly to the period from 500 AD to 1450 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weller-stearns_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-weller-stearns-3">[3]</a></sup> Beginning and ending dates might vary depending on the region, with the period beginning at the end of the previous classical period: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/End_of_the_Han_Dynasty" title="End of the Han Dynasty">Han China</a> (ending in 220 AD), the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> (in 476 AD), the <a href="/wiki/Gupta_Empire" title="Gupta Empire">Gupta Empire</a> (in 543 AD), and the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> (in 651 AD). The <a href="/wiki/College_Board" title="College Board">College Board</a> in its Pre AP World History courses classify the centuries between 600 and 1450 as the Post Classical Era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The post-classical period is one of the five or six major periods world historians use:
</p>
<ol><li>early civilization,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">classical societies</a>,</li>
<li>post-classical</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_modern" title="Early modern">early modern</a>,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Long_nineteenth_century" title="Long nineteenth century">long nineteenth century</a>, and</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_history" title="Modern history">contemporary or modern era</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weller-stearns_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-weller-stearns-3">[3]</a></sup> (Sometimes the nineteenth century and modern are combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weller-stearns_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-weller-stearns-3">[3]</a></sup>)</li></ol>
<p>Although <i>post-classical</i> is synonymous with the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> of Western Europe, the term <i>post-classical</i> is not necessarily a member of the traditional <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages#Historiography_and_periodization" title="Late Middle Ages">tripartite periodisation</a> of Western European history into <i>classical</i>, <i>middle</i> and <i>modern</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Approaches">Approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The historical field of <a href="/wiki/World_history" title="World history">world history</a>, which looks at common themes occurring across multiple cultures and regions, has enjoyed extensive development since the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> However, World History research has tended to focus on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_modern_globalization" title="Early modern globalization">early modern globalization</a> (beginning around 1500) and subsequent developments, and views post-classical history as mainly pertaining to <a href="/wiki/Afro-Eurasia" title="Afro-Eurasia">Afro-Eurasia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weller-stearns_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-weller-stearns-3">[3]</a></sup> Historians recognize the difficulties of creating a periodization and identifying common themes that include not only this region but also, for example, the Americas, since they had little contact with Afro-Eurasia before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">Columbian Exchange</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weller-stearns_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-weller-stearns-3">[3]</a></sup> Thus recent research has emphasised that "a global history of the period between 500 and 1500 is still wanting" and that "historians have only just begun to embark on a global history of the Middle Ages".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michael_Borgolte_2017_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Borgolte_2017-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>For many regions of the world, there are well established histories. Although <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Studies" title="Medieval Studies">Medieval Studies</a> in Europe tended in the nineteenth century to focus on creating histories for individual nation-states, much twentieth-century research focused, successfully, on creating an integrated history of medieval Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michael_Borgolte_2017_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Borgolte_2017-17">[16]</a></sup> The Islamic World likewise has a rich regional historiography, ranging from the fourteenth-century <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khaldun" title="Ibn Khaldun">Ibn Khaldun</a> to the twentieth-century <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Hodgson" title="Marshall Hodgson">Marshall Hodgson</a> and beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> Correspondingly, research into the network of commercial hubs which enabled goods and ideas to move between China in the East and the Atlantic islands in the West—which can be called the <a href="/wiki/Archaic_globalization" title="Archaic globalization">early history of globalization</a>—is fairly advanced; one key historian in this field is <a href="/wiki/Janet_Abu-Lughod" title="Janet Abu-Lughod">Janet Abu-Lughod</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> Understanding of communication within Sub-Saharan Africa or the Americas is, by contrast, far more limited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent history-writing, therefore, has begun to explore the possibilities of writing history covering the Old World, where Human activities were fairly interconnected, and establish its relationship with other cultural spheres, such as the Americas and Oceania. In the assessment of <a href="/wiki/James_Belich_(historian)" title="James Belich (historian)">James Belich</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Darwin_(historian)" title="John Darwin (historian)">John Darwin</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Margret_Frenz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Margret Frenz (page does not exist)">Margret Frenz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chris_Wickham" title="Chris Wickham">Chris Wickham</a>,
</p>
<blockquote><p>Global history may be boundless, but global historians are not. Global history cannot usefully mean the history of everything, everywhere, all the time. […] Three approaches […] seem to us to have real promise. One is global history as the pursuit of significant historical problems across time, space, and specialism. This can sometimes be characterized as <a href="/wiki/Comparative_history" title="Comparative history">‘comparative’ history</a>. […] Another is connectedness, including transnational relationships. […] The third approach is the study of globalization […]. Globalization is a term that needs to be rescued from the present, and salvaged for the past. To define it as always encompassing the whole planet is to mistake the current outcome for a very ancient process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>A number of commentators have pointed to the history of the earth's <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> as a useful approach to World History in the Middle Ages, noting that certain climate events had effects on all human populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_trends">Main trends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Main trends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Post-classical era saw several common developments or themes. There was the expansion and growth of civilization into new geographic areas; the rise and/or spread of the three major world, or missionary, religions; and a period of rapidly expanding trade and trade networks. While scholastic emphasis has remained on Eurasia there is a growing effort to examine the effects of these global trends on other places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> In describing cultural zones historians have identified three large self contained world regions, Afro-Eurasia, the Americas and Oceania. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[B]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_of_civilization">Growth of civilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Growth of civilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">Civilization</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piquillacta_Archaeological_site_-_street.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Piquillacta_Archaeological_site_-_street.jpg/220px-Piquillacta_Archaeological_site_-_street.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Piquillacta_Archaeological_site_-_street.jpg/330px-Piquillacta_Archaeological_site_-_street.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Piquillacta_Archaeological_site_-_street.jpg/440px-Piquillacta_Archaeological_site_-_street.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Piquillacta_Archaeological_site_-_street.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piquillacta" title="Piquillacta">Piquillacta</a>, an administrative urban center of the <a href="/wiki/Wari_Empire" title="Wari Empire">Wari Empire</a>, a South America Andean civilization that thrived from the 5th to the 8th century</div></div></div>
<p>First was the expansion and growth of civilization into new geographic areas across <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>, and western <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>. However, as noted by world historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_N._Stearns" title="Peter N. Stearns">Peter N. Stearns</a>, there were no common global political trends during the post-classical period, rather it was a period of loosely organized states and other developments, but no common political patterns emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weller-stearns_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-weller-stearns-3">[3]</a></sup> In Asia, China continued its historic <a href="/wiki/Dynastic_cycle" title="Dynastic cycle">dynastic cycle</a> and became more complex, improving its bureaucracy. The creation of the Islamic Empires established a new power in the Middle East, North Africa, and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>. Africa created the <a href="/wiki/Songhai_Empire" title="Songhai Empire">Songhai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mali_Empire" title="Mali Empire">Mali</a> kingdoms in the West. The fall of Roman civilization not only left a power vacuum for the Mediterranean and Europe, but forced certain areas to build what some historians might call new civilizations entirely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBirken1992451–461_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirken1992451–461-36">[34]</a></sup> An entirely different political system was applied in Western Europe (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>), as well as a different society (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">manorialism</a>). But the once East Roman Empire, Byzantium, retained many features of old Rome, as well as Greek and Persian similarities. Kiev Rus' and subsequently Russia began development in Eastern Europe as well. In the isolated Americas, Mesoamerica saw the building of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztec</a> Empire, while the <a href="/wiki/Andean_civilizations" title="Andean civilizations">Andean region</a> of South America saw the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Wari_Empire" title="Wari Empire">Wari Empire</a> first and the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a> later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread_of_universal_religions">Spread of universal religions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Spread of universal religions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_religion" title="History of religion">History of religion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siege_of_Acre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="615" data-file-width="650" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Siege_of_Acre.jpg/200px-Siege_of_Acre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Siege_of_Acre.jpg/300px-Siege_of_Acre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Siege_of_Acre.jpg/400px-Siege_of_Acre.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siege_of_Acre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Acre_(1189%E2%80%9391)" title="Siege of Acre (1189–91)">Siege of Acre</a> (1191; picture from 1280.) Religious wars were common in post-classical times. One of the largest was the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Religion that envisaged the possibility that all humans could be included in a universal order had emerged already in the first millennium BC, particularly with Buddhism. In the following millennium, Buddhism was joined by two other major, universalising, missionary religions, both developing from <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>: Christianity and Islam. By the end of the period, these three religions were between them widespread, and often politically dominant, across the Old World.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yuval_Noah_Harari_2014_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yuval_Noah_Harari_2014-38">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> spread from India into China and flourished there briefly before using it as a hub to spread to Japan, Korea, and Vietnam;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowman2000162–167_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowman2000162–167-39">[37]</a></sup> a similar effect occurred with Confucian revivalism in the later centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yuval_Noah_Harari_2014_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yuval_Noah_Harari_2014-38">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> had become the <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">State church of the Roman Empire</a> in 380, and continued spreading into northern and eastern Europe during the post-classical period at the expense of belief systems that Christians labelled <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompsonO'ToolePatrickPruneski2009288_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompsonO'ToolePatrickPruneski2009288-40">[38]</a></sup> An attempt was even made to incur upon the Middle East during the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>. The split of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> in Western Europe and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> in Eastern Europe encouraged religious and cultural diversity in Eurasia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> began between 610 and 632, with a series of revelations to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>. It helped unify the warring <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> clans of the Arabian peninsula and, through a rapid series of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a>, became established to the west across <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Muslim conquest of Egypt">North Africa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_conquest_of_Hispania" title="Umayyad conquest of Hispania">Iberian Peninsula</a>, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Africa" title="Islam in Africa">West Africa</a>, and to the east across <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">Persia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamicisation_of_Xinjiang" title="Islamicisation of Xinjiang">Central Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="Muslim conquests in the Indian subcontinent">India</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Islam_in_Indonesia" title="Spread of Islam in Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Outside of Eurasia, religion or otherwise a veneration of the supernatural was also used to reinforce power structures, ariculate world views and create foundatonal myths for society. Measoamerican cosmological narratives being an example. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_and_communication">Trade and communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Trade and communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trade_route" title="Trade route">Trade route</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rakaposhi_View_from_Babusar_Top.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1152" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Rakaposhi_View_from_Babusar_Top.jpg/220px-Rakaposhi_View_from_Babusar_Top.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Rakaposhi_View_from_Babusar_Top.jpg/330px-Rakaposhi_View_from_Babusar_Top.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Rakaposhi_View_from_Babusar_Top.jpg/440px-Rakaposhi_View_from_Babusar_Top.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rakaposhi_View_from_Babusar_Top.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Babusar_Pass" title="Babusar Pass">Babusar Pass</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a></div></div></div>
<p>Finally, communication and trade across <a href="/wiki/Afro-Eurasia" title="Afro-Eurasia">Afro-Eurasia</a> increased rapidly. The <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> continued to spread cultures and ideas through trade and throughout Europe, Asia, and Africa. Trade networks were established between West Europe, Byzantium, early Russia, the Islamic Empires, and the <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far Eastern</a> civilizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> In Africa the earlier introduction of the Camel allowed for a new and eventually large <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_trade" title="Trans-Saharan trade">trans-Saharan trade</a>, which connected Sub-Saharan West Africa to Eurasia. The Islamic Empires adopted many Greek, Roman, and Indian advances and spread them through the Islamic sphere of influence, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_world_contributions_to_Medieval_Europe" title="Islamic world contributions to Medieval Europe">allowing these developments to reach Europe</a>, North and West Africa, and Central Asia. Islamic sea trade helped connect these areas, including those in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>, replacing Byzantium in the latter region. The Christian Crusades into the Middle East (as well as Muslim Spain and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>) <a href="/wiki/Islamic_world_contributions_to_Medieval_Europe" title="Islamic world contributions to Medieval Europe">brought Islamic science, technology, and goods to Western Europe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompsonO'ToolePatrickPruneski2009288_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompsonO'ToolePatrickPruneski2009288-40">[38]</a></sup> Western trade into East Asia was pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>. Importantly, China began the sinicization (or Chinese influence) of regions like Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowman2000162–167_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowman2000162–167-39">[37]</a></sup> Korea, and Vietnam through trade and conquest. Finally, the growth of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> in Central Asia established safe trade such as to allow goods, cultures, ideas, and disease to spread between Asia, Europe, and Africa.
</p><p>The Americas had their own trade network, however but trade was restricted by range and scope. The Mayan network spanned across Measoamerica but lacked direct connections to the complex societies of South and North America, these zones remained seperate from one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a> some of the island chains of <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a> engaged in trade with one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> For instance, with outrigger canoes long distance communication of over 2,300 miles between <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_(island)" title="Hawaii (island)">Hawaii</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">Tahiti</a> was maintained for centuries before its disruption and seperation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup>&lt;/ref&gt;Meanwhile in Melanesia there is evidence of exchanges betweeen mainland <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a> and the Trobriand islands off of its coast 450 miles away, most lilkely for obsidian, populations moved westward until 1200, after which the network dissolved into much smaller economies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Historical_climatology" title="Historical climatology">Historical climatology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a></div><p>During Post-classical times, there is evidence that many regions of the world were affected similarly by global climate conditions; however, direct effects in temperature and precipitation varied by region. According to the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a>, changes did not all occur at once. Generally however, studies found that temperatures were relatively warmer in the 11th century, but colder by the early 17th century. The degree of climate change which occurred in all regions across the world is uncertain, as is whether such changes were all part of a global trend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup> Climate trends seemed to be more recognizable in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern</a> than in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1088" data-file-width="1449" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png/300px-2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png/450px-2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png/600px-2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstructed depth of <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a> varies among studies. Anomalies shown are from the 1950–80 reference period.</div></div></div>
<p>There are shorter climate periods that could be said roughly to account for large scale climate trends in the Post-classical Period. These include the <a href="/wiki/Late_Antique_Little_Ice_Age" title="Late Antique Little Ice Age">Late Antique Little Ice Age</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extreme_weather_events_of_535%E2%80%93536" title="Extreme weather events of 535–536">extreme weather events of 536–537</a> were likely initiated by the eruption of the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Ilopango" title="Lake Ilopango">Lake llopango caldera</a> in <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a>. Sulfate emitted into the air initiated global cooling, migrations and crop failures worldwide, possibly intensifying an already cooler time period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> Records show that the world's average temperature remained colder for at least a century afterwards.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a> from 950 to 1250 occurred mostly in the Northern Hemisphere, causing warmer summers in many areas; the high temperatures would only be surpassed by the global warming of the 20th/21st centuries. It has been hypothesized that the warmer temperatures allowed the Norse to colonize Greenland, due to ice-free waters. Outside of Europe there is evidence of warming conditions, including higher temperatures in China and major North American droughts which adversely affected numerous cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>After 1250, glaciers began to expand in Greenland, affecting its <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a>, and cooling the entire North Atlantic. In the 14th century, the growing season in Europe became unreliable; meanwhile in China the cultivation of oranges was driven southward by colder temperatures. Especially in Europe, the Little Ice Age had great cultural ramifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> It persisted until the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, long after the Post-classical Period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hendy2002_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hendy2002-53">[51]</a></sup> Its causes are unclear: possible explanations include <a href="/wiki/Solar_maximum" title="Solar maximum">sunspots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_orbit" title="Earth's orbit">orbital cycles of the Earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic activity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">ocean circulation</a>, and man-made <a href="/wiki/Population_decline" title="Population decline">population decline</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Timeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Global_Middle_Ages" title="Timeline of the Global Middle Ages">Timeline of the Global Middle Ages</a></div>
<p>This timetable gives a basic overview of states, cultures and events which transpired roughly between the years 200 and 1500. Sections are broken by political and geographic location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Eurasia">Eurasia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Eurasia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>This section explains events and trends which affected the geographic area of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>. The civilizations within this area were distinct from one another but still endured shared experiences and some development patterns
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feudalism">Feudalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Feudalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Examples_of_feudalism" title="Examples of feudalism">Examples of feudalism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mahmud_of_Ghazni_receiving_Indian_elephants_as_tribute_(Majmu_al-Tawarikh,_Hafiz_i-Abru,_Herat,_1425).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="758" data-file-width="1430" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Mahmud_of_Ghazni_receiving_Indian_elephants_as_tribute_%28Majmu_al-Tawarikh%2C_Hafiz_i-Abru%2C_Herat%2C_1425%29.jpg/220px-Mahmud_of_Ghazni_receiving_Indian_elephants_as_tribute_%28Majmu_al-Tawarikh%2C_Hafiz_i-Abru%2C_Herat%2C_1425%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Mahmud_of_Ghazni_receiving_Indian_elephants_as_tribute_%28Majmu_al-Tawarikh%2C_Hafiz_i-Abru%2C_Herat%2C_1425%29.jpg/330px-Mahmud_of_Ghazni_receiving_Indian_elephants_as_tribute_%28Majmu_al-Tawarikh%2C_Hafiz_i-Abru%2C_Herat%2C_1425%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Mahmud_of_Ghazni_receiving_Indian_elephants_as_tribute_%28Majmu_al-Tawarikh%2C_Hafiz_i-Abru%2C_Herat%2C_1425%29.jpg/440px-Mahmud_of_Ghazni_receiving_Indian_elephants_as_tribute_%28Majmu_al-Tawarikh%2C_Hafiz_i-Abru%2C_Herat%2C_1425%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mahmud_of_Ghazni_receiving_Indian_elephants_as_tribute_(Majmu_al-Tawarikh,_Hafiz_i-Abru,_Herat,_1425).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mahmud_of_Ghazni" title="Mahmud of Ghazni">Mahmud of Ghazni</a> receiving Indian elephants as tribute. Islamic conquerors often maintained preexisting feudal systems; regardless of religious differences local aristocrats often kept their positions as long as the <a href="/wiki/Jizya" title="Jizya">jizya</a> was paid.</div></div></div>
<p>In the context of global history, the label of feudalism has been used to describe any agricultural society where central authority broke down to be replaced by a warrior <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy_(class)" title="Aristocracy (class)">aristoracy</a>. Feudal societies are characterized by reliance on personal relationships with military elites, rather than a bureaucracy with a state-supported professional standing army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup> The label of feudalism has thus been used to describe many areas of Eurasia including <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Medieval Europe</a>, the Islamic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iqta" title="Iqta">iqta</a> system, Indian feudalism, and <a href="/wiki/Heian_period" title="Heian period">Heian Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> Some World Historians generalize that socities can be called feudal if authority was fragmented accompanied by a set of obligations between vassal and lord. After the 8th century, <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a> became more common across Europe. Even Byzantium, which had inherited the government of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, choose to devolve its military obligations into <a href="/wiki/Theme_(Byzantine_district)" title="Theme (Byzantine district)">themes</a> to increase the number of soldiers and ships available for military service during times of crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> There were similarities between European feudalism and the Islamic iqta as both featured landed classes of mounted warriors whose titles were granted by a monarch or sultan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> Because of these similarities, it was common for societal structures to be preserved in the face of religious upheaval; for instance, after the Islamic <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> conquered large portions of India, it imposed higher taxes but otherwise left local feudal structures in place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though most of Eurasia adopted feudalism and similar systems during this era, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_China" title="Imperial China">China</a> employed a centralized bureaucracy throughout much of the post classical period, particularly after 1000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> A major factor that distinguished China from other regions was that local leaders were reluctant to self-identify by their current location; instead, they typically displayed an ambition to unite the country in times of disunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beyond a broad generalization, the usefulness of the term 'feudalism' is debated by contemporary historians, as the daily functions of feudalism sometimes differed greatly between world regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup> Comparisons between feudal Europe and post-classical Japan have been particularly controversial. Throughout the 20th century, historians often compared medieval Europe to post-classical Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> More recently, it has been argued that, until roughly 1400, Japan balanced its decentralized military power against more centralized forms of imperial (governmental) and monastic (religious) authority. Only in the <a href="/wiki/Sengoku_period" title="Sengoku period">Sengoku period</a> did there come to be fully decentralized power dominated by private military leaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup> Still other historians reject the term feudalism outright, challenging its ability to usefully describe societies either within or outside of medieval Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mongol_Empire">Mongol Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mongol Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a></div><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DiezAlbumsArmedRiders_I.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1359" data-file-width="1658" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/DiezAlbumsArmedRiders_I.jpg/220px-DiezAlbumsArmedRiders_I.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/DiezAlbumsArmedRiders_I.jpg/330px-DiezAlbumsArmedRiders_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/DiezAlbumsArmedRiders_I.jpg/440px-DiezAlbumsArmedRiders_I.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DiezAlbumsArmedRiders_I.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mounted warriors pursue enemies. Illustration of Rashid-ad-Din's Gami' at-tawarih. Tabriz (?), 1st quarter of 14th century.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><audio class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="692" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Bookofmarcopolo1_33_pisa_64kb.ogg" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="40602" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (41 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/Bookofmarcopolo1_33_pisa_64kb.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="100136" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/5b/Bookofmarcopolo1_33_pisa_64kb.ogg/Bookofmarcopolo1_33_pisa_64kb.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bookofmarcopolo1_33_pisa_64kb.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> describes the <a href="/wiki/Toluid_Civil_War" title="Toluid Civil War">Toluid Civil War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongol_military_tactics_and_organization" title="Mongol military tactics and organization">Mongol armies</a> at that time.</div></div></div>
<p>The Mongol Empire which existed during the 13th and 14th centuries, was the largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_empires#Largest_empires_by_land_area_and_population" title="List of largest empires">continuous land empire</a> in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup> Originating in the <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, the Mongol Empire eventually stretched from <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Japan" title="Sea of Japan">Sea of Japan</a>, extending northwards into <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, eastwards and southwards into the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_plateau" title="Iranian plateau">Iranian plateau</a>, and westwards as far as the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mongol Empire emerged from the unification of nomadic tribes in the <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> homeland under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a>, who was proclaimed ruler of all Mongols in 1206. The empire grew rapidly under his rule and then under his descendants, who sent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions" title="Mongol invasions">invasions</a> in every direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> Arround 1303 The Mongol <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan Dynasty</a> combined Chinese and Islamic catagrophy to make a map that likely included all of Eurasia including western Europe, this Eurasian map is now lost but it influenced Chinese and Korean geographical knowdlege centuries later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>The vast transcontinental empire connected the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_world" title="Eastern world">east</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">west</a> with an enforced <i><a href="/wiki/Pax_Mongolica" title="Pax Mongolica">Pax Mongolica</a></i> allowing trade, technologies, commodities, and ideologies to be disseminated and exchanged across <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The empire began to split due to wars over succession, as the grandchildren of Genghis Khan disputed whether the royal line should follow from his son and initial heir <a href="/wiki/%C3%96gedei_Khan" title="Ögedei Khan">Ögedei</a>, or one of his other sons such as <a href="/wiki/Tolui" title="Tolui">Tolui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_Khan" title="Chagatai Khan">Chagatai</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Jochi" title="Jochi">Jochi</a>. After <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ngke_Khan" title="Möngke Khan">Möngke Khan</a> died, rival <i><a href="/wiki/Kurultai" title="Kurultai">kurultai</a></i> councils simultaneously elected different successors, the brothers <a href="/wiki/Ariq_B%C3%B6ke" title="Ariq Böke">Ariq Böke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a>, who then not only fought each other in the <a href="/wiki/Toluid_Civil_War" title="Toluid Civil War">Toluid Civil War</a>, but also dealt with challenges from descendants of other sons of Genghis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> Kublai successfully took power, but civil war ensued as Kublai sought unsuccessfully to regain control of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chagatai_khans" title="Chagatai khans">Chagatayid</a> and <a href="/wiki/House_of_%C3%96gedei" title="House of Ögedei">Ögedeid</a> families.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LetterOljeituToPhilipLeBel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="801" data-file-width="2574" decoding="async" height="68" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/LetterOljeituToPhilipLeBel.jpg/220px-LetterOljeituToPhilipLeBel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/LetterOljeituToPhilipLeBel.jpg/330px-LetterOljeituToPhilipLeBel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/LetterOljeituToPhilipLeBel.jpg/440px-LetterOljeituToPhilipLeBel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LetterOljeituToPhilipLeBel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Letter from the Mongolian-Persian <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a> to France, 1305. The Chinese style stamp was used outside China as the official symbol of the Khans and their messengers</div></div></div><p>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ain_Jalut" title="Battle of Ain Jalut">Battle of Ain Jalut</a> in 1260 marked the high-water point of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_conquests" title="Mongol conquests">Mongol conquests</a> and was the first time a Mongol advance had ever been beaten back in direct combat on the battlefield. Though the Mongols launched many more invasions into the Levant, briefly occupying it and raiding as far as Gaza after a decisive victory at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Wadi_al-Khazandar" title="Battle of Wadi al-Khazandar">Battle of Wadi al-Khazandar</a> in 1299, they withdrew due to various geopolitical factors.
</p><p>By the time of Kublai's death in 1294, the Mongol Empire had fractured into four separate khanates or empires, each pursuing its own separate interests and objectives: the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a> khanate in the northwest; the <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_Khanate" title="Chagatai Khanate">Chagatai Khanate</a> in the west; the <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a> in the southwest; and the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> based in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-China_p413_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-China_p413-80">[78]</a></sup> In 1304, the three western khanates briefly accepted the nominal suzerainty of the Yuan dynasty,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> but it was later overthrown by the <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> in 1368.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-83">[81]</a></sup> The Genghisid rulers returned to Mongolia homeland and continued rule in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Yuan_dynasty" title="Northern Yuan dynasty">Northern Yuan dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-83">[81]</a></sup> All of the original Mongol Khanates collapsed by 1500, but smaller successor states remained independent until the 1700s. Descendants of <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_Khan" title="Chagatai Khan">Chagatai Khan</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> that ruled much of India in <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern</a> times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-:6-83">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The conquests and the interactions the Mongol Empire had with western Eurasia are one of the more comprehensively researched areas for historians looking to define a globalized middle ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Silk_Road">The Silk Road</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: The Silk Road">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Central_Asia" title="History of Central Asia">History of Central Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Siberia" title="History of Siberia">History of Siberia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="470" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg/170px-Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg/255px-Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg/340px-Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Central Asian Buddhist Monks, the Silk Road allowed for the exchange for ideas as well as goods. A Central Asian <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> teacher possibly <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdian</a> instructs a monk. Dated from the 9th century near <a href="/wiki/Turpan" title="Turpan">Turfan, Xinxiang, China</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><audio class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="273" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Bookofmarcopolo1_chap22_pisa_64kb.ogg" id="mwe_player_1" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="51388" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (51 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/Bookofmarcopolo1_chap22_pisa_64kb.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/></audio> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bookofmarcopolo1_chap22_pisa_64kb.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> describing <a href="/wiki/Khanbaliq" title="Khanbaliq">Khanbaliq's</a> (Beijing's) role in the Silk Road</div></div></div>
<p>The Silk Road was a Eurasian trade route that played a large role in global communication and interaction. It stimulated cultural exchange; encouraged the learning of new languages; resulted in the trade of many goods, such as silk, gold, and spices; and also spread religion and disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian20001–21_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian20001–21-85">[83]</a></sup> It is even claimed by some historians – such as <a href="/wiki/Andre_Gunder_Frank" title="Andre Gunder Frank">Andre Gunder Frank</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Hardy_McNeill" title="William Hardy McNeill">William Hardy McNeill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jerry_H._Bentley" title="Jerry H. Bentley">Jerry H. Bentley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Hodgson" title="Marshall Hodgson">Marshall Hodgson</a> – that the Afro-Eurasian world was loosely united culturally, and that the Silk Road was fundamental to this unity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian20001–21_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian20001–21-85">[83]</a></sup> This major trade route began with the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> of China, connecting it to the Roman Empire and any regions in between or nearby. At this time, Central Asia exported horses, <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jade" title="Jade">jade</a> into China for the latter's silk; the Romans would trade for the Chinese commodity as well, offering wine in return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowman2000101_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowman2000101-86">[84]</a></sup> The Silk Road would often decline and rise again in trade from the Iron Age to the Postclassical Era. Following one such decline, it was reopened in Central Asia by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a> General <a href="/wiki/Ban_Chao" title="Ban Chao">Ban Chao</a> during the 1st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowman2000568_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowman2000568-87">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Silk Road was also a major factor in spreading religion across Afro-Eurasia. Muslim teachings from Arabia and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> reached East Asia. Buddhism spread from India, to China, to Central Asia. One significant development in the spread of Buddhism was the carving of the <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a> School in the cities of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxila_(ancient)" title="Taxila (ancient)">ancient Taxila</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peshwar" title="Peshwar">Peshwar</a>, allegedly in the mid 1st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowman2000568_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowman2000568-87">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The route was vulnerable to spreading plague. The <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">Plague of Justinian</a> originated in East Asia and had a major outbreak in Europe in 542 causing the deaths of a quarter of the Mediterranean's population. Trade between Europe, Africa and Asia along the route was at least partially responsible for spreading the plague.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> There is a popular theory that the Black Death was caused by the Mongol conquests. The claim is that the direct link that it opened between the East and West provided the path for rats and fleas that carried the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> Although there is no concrete historical evidence to this theory, the plague is considered endemic on the steppe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>There were vulnerabilities as well to changing political situations. The rise of Islam changed the Silk Road, because Muslim rulers generally closed the Silk Road to <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christian Europe</a> to an extent Europe would be cut off from Asia for centuries. Specifically, the political developments that affected the Silk Road included the emergence of the Turks, the political movements of the Sasanian and Byzantine empires, and the rise of the Arabs, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Song_Tapestry.jpg"><img alt="Chinese-Song era tapestry" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2861" data-file-width="1516" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Song_Tapestry.jpg/140px-Song_Tapestry.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Song_Tapestry.jpg/210px-Song_Tapestry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Song_Tapestry.jpg/280px-Song_Tapestry.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Song_Tapestry.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese-Song-era tapestry, Chinese Silk was carried west over large distances and sold for large profits.</div></div></div><p>
The Silk Road flourished again in the 13th century during the reign of the Mongol Empire, which through conquest had brought stability in Central Asia comparable to the <a href="/wiki/Pax_Romana" title="Pax Romana">Pax Romana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarraclough2003146_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarraclough2003146-92">[90]</a></sup> It was claimed by a Muslim historian that Central Asia was peaceful and safe to transverse </p><blockquote><p>"(Central Asia) enjoyed such a peace that a man might have journeyed from the land of sunrise to the land of sunset with a golden platter upon his head without suffering the least violence from anyone."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStearnsAdasSchwartzGilbert2011321_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStearnsAdasSchwartzGilbert2011321-93">[91]</a></sup> </p></blockquote>
<p>As such, trade and communication between Europe, East Asia, South Asia, and the Middle East required little effort. Handicraft production, art, and scholarship prospered, and wealthy merchants enjoyed cosmopolitan cities. Notable Travelers including <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Battuta" title="Ibn Battuta">Ibn Battuta</a> , <a href="/wiki/Rabban_Bar_Sauma" title="Rabban Bar Sauma">Rabban Bar Sauma</a>  and <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> travelled across North Africa and Eurasia  freely, those that left accounts of their experiences inspired future  adventurers. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStearnsAdasSchwartzGilbert2011321_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStearnsAdasSchwartzGilbert2011321-93">[91]</a></sup>. In addition to commerical travel was the esteem of <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">Pilgrimage</a> that existed across all of Afro-Eurrasia, in the words of world historian <a href="/wiki/R._I._Moore" title="R. I. Moore">R. I. Moore</a> "if any single institution ‘made’ the Eurasian Middle Ages it was pilgrimage" <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Silk Road trade played a role in spreading the infamous <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>. Originating in China, the <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a> was spread by Mongol warriors catapulting diseased corpses into enemy towns in the <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a>. The disease, spread by rats, was carried by merchant ships sailing across the Mediterranean that brought the plague back to Sicily, causing an <a href="/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">epidemic</a> in 1347.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompsonO'ToolePatrickPruneski2009310_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompsonO'ToolePatrickPruneski2009310-96">[94]</a></sup> Nevertheless, after the 15th century, the Silk Road disappeared from regular use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarraclough2003146_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarraclough2003146-92">[90]</a></sup> This was primarily a result from the growing sea travel pioneered by Europeans, which allowed the trade of goods by sailing around the southern tip of Africa and into the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarraclough2003146_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarraclough2003146-92">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Post-classical_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Science#Post-classical_science" title="History of Science">History of Science § Post-classical science</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_and_technology_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="History of science and technology in the Indian subcontinent">History of science and technology in the Indian subcontinent</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_and_technology_in_China" title="History of science and technology in China">History of science and technology in China</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2083" data-file-width="2130" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg/220px-Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg/330px-Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg/440px-Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Westerner and Arab practicing geometry 15th century manuscript</div></div></div>
<p>The term <i>post-classical science</i> is often used in academic circles and in college courses to combine the study of <a href="/wiki/European_science_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="European science in the Middle Ages">medieval European science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Science in the medieval Islamic world">medieval Islamic science</a> due to their interactions with one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup> However scientific knowledge also spread westward by trade and war from Eastern Eurasia, particularly from China by Arabs. The Islamic World also took medical knowledge from <a href="/wiki/Indian_influence_on_Islamic_science" title="Indian influence on Islamic science">South Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the case of the Western World and in Islamic realms much emphasis was placed on preserving the rationalist Greek Tradition of figures such as <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>. In the context of science within Islam there are questions as to whether Islamic Scientists simply preserved accomplishments from <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Antiquity</a> or built upon earlier Greek advances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup> Regardless, Classical European Science was brought back to the Christian Kingdoms due to the experience of the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergerIsraelMillerParkinson2016451_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergerIsraelMillerParkinson2016451-101">[99]</a></sup>
